<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Riemann hypothesis</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">"Critical line" redirects here. For the notion in phase transition, see <a href="/wiki/Critical_line_(thermodynamics)" title="Critical line (thermodynamics)">Critical line (thermodynamics)</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For the musical term, see <a href="/wiki/Riemannian_theory" title="Riemannian theory">Riemannian theory</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RiemannCriticalLine.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/RiemannCriticalLine.svg/220px-RiemannCriticalLine.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/RiemannCriticalLine.svg/330px-RiemannCriticalLine.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/RiemannCriticalLine.svg/440px-RiemannCriticalLine.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RiemannCriticalLine.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The real part (red) and imaginary part (blue) of the Riemann zeta function along the critical line Re(<i>s</i>) = 1/2. The first non-trivial zeros can be seen at Im(<i>s</i>) = ±14.135, ±21.022 and ±25.011.</div>

<p>In mathematics, the <b>Riemann hypothesis</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Conjecture" title="Conjecture">conjecture</a> that the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a> has its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Root_of_a_function" title="Root of a function">zeros</a> only at the negative even integers and <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex numbers</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_part" title="Real part">real part</a> <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span>. It was proposed by <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann" title="Bernhard Riemann">Bernhard Riemann</a> (<a href="#CITEREFRiemann1859">1859</a>), after whom it is named. The name is also used for some closely related analogues, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riemann_hypothesis_for_curves_over_finite_fields" title="Riemann hypothesis for curves over finite fields">Riemann hypothesis for curves over finite fields</a>.</p>
<p>The Riemann hypothesis implies results about the distribution of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_numbers" title="Prime numbers">prime numbers</a>. Along with suitable generalizations, some mathematicians consider it the most important unresolved problem in <a href="/wiki/Pure_mathematics" title="Pure mathematics">pure mathematics</a> (<a href="#CITEREFBombieri2000">Bombieri 2000</a>). The Riemann hypothesis, along with <a href="/wiki/Goldbach%27s_conjecture" title="Goldbach's conjecture">Goldbach's conjecture</a>, is part of <a href="/wiki/Hilbert%27s_eighth_problem" title="Hilbert's eighth problem">Hilbert's eighth problem</a> in <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a>'s list of <a href="/wiki/Hilbert%27s_problems" title="Hilbert's problems">23 unsolved problems</a>; it is also one of the <a href="/wiki/Clay_Mathematics_Institute" title="Clay Mathematics Institute">Clay Mathematics Institute</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Prize_Problems" title="Millennium Prize Problems">Millennium Prize Problems</a>.</p>
<p>The Riemann zeta function ζ(<i>s</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function (mathematics)">function</a> whose <a href="/wiki/Argument_of_a_function" title="Argument of a function">argument</a> <i>s</i> may be any <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex number</a> other than 1, and whose values are also complex. It has zeros at the negative even integers; that is, ζ(<i>s</i>) = 0 when <i>s</i> is one of −2, −4, −6, .... These are called its <b>trivial zeros</b>. However, the negative even integers are not the only values for which the zeta function is zero. The other ones are called <i>non-trivial zeros</i>. The Riemann hypothesis is concerned with the locations of these non-trivial zeros, and states that:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The real part of every non-trivial zero of the Riemann zeta function is <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span>.</dd>
</dl>
<p>Thus, if the hypothesis is correct, all the non-trivial zeros lie on the <b>critical line</b> consisting of the complex numbers <span class="nowrap"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span> + <i>i<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap"> </span>t</i>,</span> where <i>t</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real number</a> and <i>i</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_unit" title="Imaginary unit">imaginary unit</a>.</p>
<p>There are several nontechnical books on the Riemann hypothesis, such as <a href="#CITEREFDerbyshire2003">Derbyshire (2003)</a>, <a href="#CITEREFRockmore2005">Rockmore (2005)</a>, (Sabbagh <a href="#CITEREFSabbagh2003a">2003a</a>, <a href="#CITEREFSabbagh2003b">2003b</a>), <a href="#CITEREFdu_Sautoy2003">du Sautoy (2003)</a>. The books <a href="#CITEREFEdwards1974">Edwards (1974)</a>, <a href="#CITEREFPatterson1988">Patterson (1988)</a>, <a href="#CITEREFBorweinChoiRooneyWeirathmueller2008">Borwein et al. (2008)</a> and <a href="#CITEREFMazurStein2015">Mazur &amp; Stein (2015)</a> give mathematical introductions, while <a href="#CITEREFTitchmarsh1986">Titchmarsh (1986)</a>, <a href="#CITEREFIvi.C4.871985">Ivić (1985)</a> and <a href="#CITEREFKaratsubaVoronin1992">Karatsuba &amp; Voronin (1992)</a> are advanced monographs.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Riemann_zeta_function">Riemann zeta function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Riemann zeta function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a> is defined for complex <i>s</i> with real part greater than 1 by the <a href="/wiki/Absolute_convergence" title="Absolute convergence">absolutely convergent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infinite_series" title="Infinite series">infinite series</a></p>

</dl>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\zeta(s) = \prod_{p \text{ prime}} \frac{1}{1-p^{-s}}= \frac{1}{1-2^{-s}}\cdot\frac{1}{1-3^{-s}}\cdot\frac{1}{1-5^{-s}}\cdot\frac{1}{1-7^{-s}} \cdots \frac{1}{1-p^{-s}} \cdots" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/71c1b7e9bee22cf3b618d1cffc4acdc783571ca6" style="vertical-align: -3.338ex; width:77.896ex; height:6.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where the <a href="/wiki/Infinite_product" title="Infinite product">infinite product</a> extends over all prime numbers <i>p</i>, and again converges for complex <i>s</i> with real part greater than 1. The convergence of the Euler product shows that ζ(<i>s</i>) has no zeros in this region, as none of the factors have zeros.</p>
<p>The Riemann hypothesis discusses zeros outside the region of convergence of this series and Euler product. To make sense of the hypothesis, it is necessary to <a href="/wiki/Analytic_continuation" title="Analytic continuation">analytically continue</a> the function to give it a definition that is valid for all complex <i>s</i>. This can be done by expressing it in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Dirichlet_eta_function" title="Dirichlet eta function">Dirichlet eta function</a> as follows. If the real part of <i>s</i> is greater than one, then the zeta function satisfies</p>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\left(1-\frac{2}{2^s}\right)\zeta(s) = \sum_{n=1}^\infty \frac{(-1)^{n+1}}{n^s} = \frac{1}{1^s} - \frac{1}{2^s} + \frac{1}{3^s} - \cdots." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/474c15c0041592b67212bb850cbe6e7d0a3cc623" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:55.269ex; height:7.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>However, the series on the right converges not just when the real part of <i>s</i> is greater than one, but more generally whenever <i>s</i> has positive real part. Thus, this alternative series extends the zeta function from <span class="nowrap">Re(<i>s</i>) &gt; 1</span> to the larger domain <span class="nowrap">Re(<i>s</i>) &gt; 0</span>, excluding the zeros <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="1-2/2^s" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1574b3084d18ec8997f0f1537434eb55f96d6715" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.553ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> (see <a href="/wiki/Dirichlet_eta_function#Landau.27s_problem_with_zeta.28s.29.3Deta.28s.29.2F0_and_solutions" title="Dirichlet eta function">Dirichlet eta function</a>). The zeta function can be extended to these values, as well, by taking limits, giving a finite value for all values of <i>s</i> with positive real part except for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_pole" title="Simple pole">simple pole</a> at <i>s</i> = 1.</p>
<p>In the strip <span class="nowrap">0 &lt; Re(<i>s</i>) &lt; 1</span> the zeta function satisfies the <a href="/wiki/Functional_equation" title="Functional equation">functional equation</a></p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mi>s</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mn>2</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>s</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<msup>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>s</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mi>s</mi>
</mrow>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \zeta (s)=2^{s}\pi ^{s-1}\ \sin \left({\frac {\pi s}{2}}\right)\ \Gamma (1-s)\ \zeta (1-s).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\zeta(s) = 2^s\pi^{s-1}\ \sin\left(\frac{\pi s}{2}\right)\ \Gamma(1-s)\ \zeta(1-s)." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/37b4eab44e38b188e9cdde9f8d8344ca79a980ec" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:42.028ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>One may then define ζ(<i>s</i>) for all remaining nonzero complex numbers <i>s</i> by assuming that this equation holds outside the strip as well, and letting ζ(<i>s</i>) equal the right-hand side of the equation whenever <i>s</i> has non-positive real part. If <i>s</i> is a negative even integer then ζ(<i>s</i>) = 0 because the factor sin(π<i>s</i>/2) vanishes; these are the <b>trivial zeros</b> of the zeta function. (If <i>s</i> is a positive even integer this argument does not apply because the zeros of the <a href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sine</a> function are cancelled by the poles of the <a href="/wiki/Gamma_function" title="Gamma function">gamma function</a> as it takes negative integer arguments.) The value <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_%2B_1_%2B_1_%2B_1_%2B_%C2%B7_%C2%B7_%C2%B7" title="1 + 1 + 1 + 1 + · · ·">ζ(0) = −1/2</a> is not determined by the functional equation, but is the limiting value of ζ(<i>s</i>) as <i>s</i> approaches zero. The functional equation also implies that the zeta function has no zeros with negative real part other than the trivial zeros, so all non-trivial zeros lie in the <b>critical strip</b> where <i>s</i> has real part between 0 and 1.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"<span lang="de" xml:lang="de">…<i>es ist sehr wahrscheinlich, dass alle Wurzeln reell sind. Hiervon wäre allerdings ein strenger Beweis zu wünschen; ich habe indess die Aufsuchung desselben nach einigen flüchtigen vergeblichen Versuchen vorläufig bei Seite gelassen, da er für den nächsten Zweck meiner Untersuchung entbehrlich schien</i>.</span>"<br/>
<br/>
("…it is very probable that all roots are real. Of course one would wish for a rigorous proof here; I have for the time being, after some fleeting vain attempts, provisionally put aside the search for this, as it appears dispensable for the immediate objective of my investigation.")</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Riemann's statement of the Riemann hypothesis, from (<a href="#CITEREFRiemann1859">Riemann 1859</a>). (He was discussing a version of the zeta function, modified so that its roots (zeros) are real rather than on the critical line.)</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Riemann's original motivation for studying the zeta function and its zeros was their occurrence in his <a href="/wiki/Explicit_formulae_(L-function)" title="Explicit formulae (L-function)">explicit formula</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Prime-counting_function" title="Prime-counting function">number of primes</a> π(<i>x</i>) less than a given number <i>x</i>, which he published in his 1859 paper <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Number_of_Primes_Less_Than_a_Given_Magnitude" title="On the Number of Primes Less Than a Given Magnitude">On the Number of Primes Less Than a Given Magnitude</a></i>. His formula was given in terms of the related function</p>

<p>which counts the primes and prime powers up to <i>x</i>, counting a prime power <i>p</i><sup><i>n</i></sup> as 1/<i>n</i> of a prime. </dl>
<p>The summation in Riemann's formula is not <a href="/wiki/Absolute_convergence" title="Absolute convergence">absolutely convergent</a>, but may be evaluated by taking the zeros ρ in order of the absolute value of their imaginary part. The function Li occurring in the first term is the (unoffset) <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_integral_function" title="Logarithmic integral function">logarithmic integral function</a> given by the <a href="/wiki/Cauchy_principal_value" title="Cauchy principal value">Cauchy principal value</a> of the divergent integral</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<msubsup>
<mo>∫<!-- ∫ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \operatorname {Li} (x)=\int _{0}^{x}{\frac {dt}{\log(t)}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\operatorname{Li}(x) = \int_0^x\frac{dt}{\log(t)}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c99d47ef9fc9a768cbe89b01e3867e44ec638a8e" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:19.618ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The terms Li(<i>x</i><sup><i>ρ</i></sup>) involving the zeros of the zeta function need some care in their definition as Li has branch points at 0 and 1, and are defined (for <i>x</i> &gt; 1) by analytic continuation in the complex variable <i>ρ</i> in the region Re(<i>ρ</i>) &gt; 0, i.e. they should be considered as <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Exponential_integral" title="Exponential integral">Ei</a>(<i>ρ</i> ln <i>x</i>)</span>. The other terms also correspond to zeros: the dominant term Li(<i>x</i>) comes from the pole at <i>s</i> = 1, considered as a zero of multiplicity −1, and the remaining small terms come from the trivial zeros. For some graphs of the sums of the first few terms of this series see <a href="#CITEREFRieselG.C3.B6hl1970">Riesel &amp; Göhl (1970)</a> or <a href="#CITEREFZagier1977">Zagier (1977)</a>.</p>
<p>This formula says that the zeros of the Riemann zeta function control the oscillations of primes around their "expected" positions. Riemann knew that the non-trivial zeros of the zeta function were symmetrically distributed about the line <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1/2 + <i>it</i>,</span> and he knew that all of its non-trivial zeros must lie in the range <span class="nowrap">0 ≤ Re(<i>s</i>) ≤ 1.</span> He checked that a few of the zeros lay on the critical line with real part 1/2 and suggested that they all do; this is the Riemann hypothesis.</p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>The result has caught the imagination of most mathematicians because it is so unexpected, connecting two seemingly unrelated areas in mathematics; namely, <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a>, which is the study of the discrete, and <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex analysis</a>, which deals with continuous processes. (<a href="#CITEREFDavid_M._Burton2006">David M. Burton 2006</a>, p. 376)</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences">Consequences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Consequences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The practical uses of the Riemann hypothesis include many propositions known true under the Riemann hypothesis, and some that can be shown equivalent to the Riemann hypothesis.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_of_prime_numbers">Distribution of prime numbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Distribution of prime numbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riemann%27s_explicit_formula" title="Riemann's explicit formula">Riemann's explicit formula</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_counting_function" title="Prime counting function">the number of primes less than a given number</a> in terms of a sum over the zeros of the Riemann zeta function says that the magnitude of the oscillations of primes around their expected position is controlled by the real parts of the zeros of the zeta function. In particular the error term in the <a href="/wiki/Prime_number_theorem" title="Prime number theorem">prime number theorem</a> is closely related to the position of the zeros: for example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supremum" title="Supremum">supremum</a> of real parts of the zeros is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infimum" title="Infimum">infimum</a> of numbers β such that the error is O(<i>x</i><sup>β</sup>) (<a href="#CITEREFIngham1932">Ingham 1932</a>).</p>
<p><a href="#CITEREFvon_Koch1901">Von Koch (1901)</a> proved that the Riemann hypothesis implies the "best possible" bound for the error of the prime number theorem. A precise version of Koch's result, due to <a href="#CITEREFSchoenfeld1976">Schoenfeld (1976)</a>, says that the Riemann hypothesis implies</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>Li</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>&lt;</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow>
<mn>8</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mi>x</mi>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mi>log</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>,</mo>
<mspace width="2em"></mspace>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext>for all </mtext>
</mrow>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>≥<!-- ≥ --></mo>
<mn>2657</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle |\pi (x)-\operatorname {Li} (x)|&lt;{\frac {1}{8\pi }}{\sqrt {x}}\log(x),\qquad {\text{for all }}x\geq 2657,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle |\pi (x)-\operatorname {Li} (x)|&lt;{\frac {1}{8\pi }}{\sqrt {x}}\log(x),\qquad {\text{for all }}x\geq 2657,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/12ed47be515e8ea3aa8f9f8c24b049b445b82a29" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:52.23ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where π(<i>x</i>) is the <a href="/wiki/Prime-counting_function" title="Prime-counting function">prime-counting function</a>, and log(<i>x</i>) is the <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a> of <i>x</i>.</p>
<p><a href="#CITEREFSchoenfeld1976">Schoenfeld (1976)</a> also showed that the Riemann hypothesis implies</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>ψ<!-- ψ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>

</dl>
<p>where ψ(<i>x</i>) is <a href="/wiki/Chebyshev_function" title="Chebyshev function">Chebyshev's second function</a>.</p>
<p>In 2015, Dudek proved that the Riemann hypothesis implies there is a prime <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="p" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.079ex; width:1.259ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> satisfying</p>
<blockquote>
<p><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>x</mi>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mfrac>

<mi>x</mi>
</msqrt>

<mi>log</mi>

<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x&lt;p\leq x+{\frac {4}{\pi }}{\sqrt {x}}\log x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle x&lt;p\leq x+{\frac {4}{\pi }}{\sqrt {x}}\log x}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ce1acb6bda43b034701bcbd98a7e4b5edfd29ba3" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:23.511ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></p>
</blockquote>
<p>for all <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle x\geq 2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e5e4cf59b5136062d043f79c2bf0c6af0443c1a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:5.622ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> This is an explicit version of a theorem of Cramer.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_arithmetic_functions">Growth of arithmetic functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Growth of arithmetic functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Riemann hypothesis implies strong bounds on the growth of many other arithmetic functions, in addition to the primes counting function above.</p>
<p>One example involves the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6bius_function" title="Möbius function">Möbius function</a> μ. The statement that the equation</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow>
<mi>ζ<!-- ζ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>s</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<munderover>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
</mrow>
</munderover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
<msup>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>s</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{\zeta (s)}}=\sum _{n=1}^{\infty }{\frac {\mu (n)}{n^{s}}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\frac{1}{\zeta(s)} = \sum_{n=1}^\infty \frac{\mu(n)}{n^s}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/44321887183e072812ddd22b8ed103e08ced1e0d" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:17.218ex; height:7.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>is valid for every <i>s</i> with real part greater than 1/2, with the sum on the right hand side converging, is equivalent to the Riemann hypothesis. From this we can also conclude that if the <a href="/wiki/Mertens_function" title="Mertens function">Mertens function</a> is defined by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>M</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<munder>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>≤<!-- ≤ --></mo>
<mi>x</mi>
</mrow>
</munder>
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle M(x)=\sum _{n\leq x}\mu (n)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="M(x) = \sum_{n \le x} \mu(n)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/056958331c498fd0fdb9b4b76c1101a97f352d0b" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:17.132ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>then the claim that</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>M</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>O</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msup>
<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>ε<!-- ε --></mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle M(x)=O(x^{{\frac {1}{2}}+\varepsilon })}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="M(x) = O(x^{\frac{1}{2}+\varepsilon})" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8ce59c959761073f9c460a9dba37b7b8e186b1a6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.487ex; height:4.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>for every positive ε is equivalent to the Riemann hypothesis (<a href="/wiki/John_Edensor_Littlewood" title="John Edensor Littlewood">J.E. Littlewood</a>, 1912; see for instance: paragraph 14.25 in <a href="#CITEREFTitchmarsh1986">Titchmarsh (1986)</a>). (For the meaning of these symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Big_O_notation" title="Big O notation">Big O notation</a>.) The determinant of the order <i>n</i> <a href="/wiki/Redheffer_matrix" title="Redheffer matrix">Redheffer matrix</a> is equal to <i>M</i>(<i>n</i>), so the Riemann hypothesis can also be stated as a condition on the growth of these determinants. The Riemann hypothesis puts a rather tight bound on the growth of <i>M</i>, since <a href="#CITEREFOdlyzkote_Riele1985">Odlyzko &amp; te Riele (1985)</a> disproved the slightly stronger <a href="/wiki/Mertens_conjecture" title="Mertens conjecture">Mertens conjecture</a></p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>M</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>≤<!-- ≤ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mi>x</mi>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle |M(x)|\leq {\sqrt {x}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="|M(x)| \le \sqrt x." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9a932e1ee98741315c0f17350dc706e3d46f4d8a" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:13.99ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The Riemann hypothesis is equivalent to many other conjectures about the rate of growth of other arithmetic functions aside from μ(<i>n</i>). A typical example is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robin%27s_theorem" title="Robin's theorem">Robin's theorem</a> (<a href="#CITEREFRobin1984">Robin 1984</a>), which states that if σ(<i>n</i>) is the <a href="/wiki/Divisor_function" title="Divisor function">divisor function</a>, given by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>σ<!-- σ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<munder>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>d</mi>
<mo>∣<!-- ∣ --></mo>
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</munder>
<mi>d</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \sigma (n)=\sum _{d\mid n}d}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\sigma(n) = \sum_{d\mid n} d" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7a6dfd779b351698b174a64e351ff24363f53ec4" style="vertical-align: -3.671ex; width:12.663ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>then</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>σ<!-- σ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>&lt;</mo>
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>γ<!-- γ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mi>n</mi>
<mi>log</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>log</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>n</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \sigma (n)&lt;e^{\gamma }n\log \log n}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\sigma(n) &lt; e^\gamma n \log \log n" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e169504a533384cc31b217ecb6749fc634a22fd7" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:19.888ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>for all <i>n</i> &gt; 5040 if and only if the Riemann hypothesis is true, where γ is the <a href="/wiki/Euler%E2%80%93Mascheroni_constant" title="Euler–Mascheroni constant">Euler–Mascheroni constant</a>.</p>
<p>Another example was found by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Franel" title="Jérôme Franel">Jérôme Franel</a>, and extended by <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Landau" title="Edmund Landau">Landau</a> (see <a href="#CITEREFFranelLandau1924">Franel &amp; Landau (1924)</a>). The Riemann hypothesis is equivalent to several statements showing that the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Farey_sequence" title="Farey sequence">Farey sequence</a> are fairly regular. One such equivalence is as follows: if <i>F</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> is the Farey sequence of order <i>n</i>, beginning with 1/<i>n</i> and up to 1/1, then the claim that for all ε &gt; 0</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<munderover>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>m</mi>
</mrow>
</munderover>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<msub>
<mi>F</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>m</mi>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>O</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<msup>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>ϵ<!-- ϵ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \sum _{i=1}^{m}|F_{n}(i)-{\tfrac {i}{m}}|=O(n^{{\frac {1}{2}}+\epsilon })}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\sum_{i=1}^m|F_n(i) - \tfrac{i}{m}| = O(n^{\frac{1}{2}+\epsilon})" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3725f8d3db834f204fa3ef4bf7eb7427ff088201" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:27.409ex; height:7.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>is equivalent to the Riemann hypothesis. Here</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>m</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<munderover>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</munderover>
<mi>ϕ<!-- ϕ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle m=\sum _{i=1}^{n}\phi (i)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="m = \sum_{i=1}^n\phi(i)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f525c950480425b7dce5c7d3928a215b3053e1d3" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:12.951ex; height:7.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>is the number of terms in the Farey sequence of order <i>n</i>.</p>
<p>For an example from <a href="/wiki/Group_theory" title="Group theory">group theory</a>, if <i>g</i>(<i>n</i>) is <a href="/wiki/Landau%27s_function" title="Landau's function">Landau's function</a> given by the maximal order of elements of the <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_group" title="Symmetric group">symmetric group</a> <i>S</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> of degree <i>n</i>, then <a href="#CITEREFMassiasNicolasRobin1988">Massias, Nicolas &amp; Robin (1988)</a> showed that the Riemann hypothesis is equivalent to the bound</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>log</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>g</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>&lt;</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<msup>
<mi>Li</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \log g(n)&lt;{\sqrt {\operatorname {Li} ^{-1}(n)}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\log g(n) &lt; \sqrt{\operatorname{Li}^{-1}(n)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b1eb21548aa954dda976377acda88a686e8aead5" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:20.899ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>for all sufficiently large <i>n</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lindel.C3.B6f_hypothesis_and_growth_of_the_zeta_function">Lindelöf hypothesis and growth of the zeta function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Lindelöf hypothesis and growth of the zeta function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Riemann hypothesis has various weaker consequences as well; one is the <b><a href="/wiki/Lindel%C3%B6f_hypothesis" title="Lindelöf hypothesis">Lindelöf hypothesis</a></b> on the rate of growth of the zeta function on the critical line, which says that, for any <i>ε</i> &gt; 0,</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ζ<!-- ζ --></mi>
<mrow>
<mo>(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>

</mrow>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \zeta \left({\frac {1}{2}}+it\right)=O(t^{\varepsilon }),}</annotation>
</semantics>

</dl>
<p>as <i>t</i> → ∞.</p>

</dl>
<p>so the growth rate of ζ(1+<i>it</i>) and its inverse would be known up to a factor of 2 (<a href="#CITEREFTitchmarsh1986">Titchmarsh 1986</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Large_prime_gap_conjecture">Large prime gap conjecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Large prime gap conjecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The prime number theorem implies that on average, the <a href="/wiki/Prime_gap" title="Prime gap">gap</a> between the prime <i>p</i> and its successor is log <i>p</i>. However, some gaps between primes may be much larger than the average. <a href="/wiki/Harald_Cram%C3%A9r" title="Harald Cramér">Cramér</a> proved that, assuming the Riemann hypothesis, every gap is <i>O</i>(√<i>p</i> log <i>p</i>). This is a case in which even the best bound that can be proved using the Riemann Hypothesis is far weaker than what seems true: <a href="/wiki/Cram%C3%A9r%27s_conjecture" title="Cramér's conjecture">Cramér's conjecture</a> implies that every gap is <i>O</i>((log <i>p</i>)<sup>2</sup>), which, while larger than the average gap, is far smaller than the bound implied by the Riemann hypothesis. Numerical evidence supports Cramér's conjecture (<a href="#CITEREFNicely1999">Nicely 1999</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criteria_equivalent_to_the_Riemann_hypothesis">Criteria equivalent to the Riemann hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Criteria equivalent to the Riemann hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many statements equivalent to the Riemann hypothesis have been found, though so far none of them have led to much progress in proving (or disproving) it. Some typical examples are as follows. (Others involve the <a href="/wiki/Divisor_function#Approximate_growth_rate" title="Divisor function">divisor function</a> σ(<i>n</i>).)</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riesz_criterion" title="Riesz criterion">Riesz criterion</a> was given by <a href="#CITEREFRiesz1916">Riesz (1916)</a>, to the effect that the bound</p>
<dl>

<p><a href="#CITEREFNyman1950">Nyman (1950)</a> proved that the Riemann Hypothesis is true if and only if the space of functions of the form</p>

<p>has no non-trivial bounded solutions <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p><a href="/wiki/Weil%27s_criterion" title="Weil's criterion">Weil's criterion</a> is the statement that the positivity of a certain function is equivalent to the Riemann hypothesis. Related is <a href="/wiki/Li%27s_criterion" title="Li's criterion">Li's criterion</a>, a statement that the positivity of a certain sequence of numbers is equivalent to the Riemann hypothesis.</p>
<p><a href="#CITEREFSpeiser1934">Speiser (1934)</a> proved that the Riemann hypothesis is equivalent to the statement that <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \zeta '(s)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\zeta'(s)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/39cf60f4412cec0a1917e0d4db4bdf6848bf6e07" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.752ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span>, the derivative of ζ(<i>s</i>), has no zeros in the strip</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mi mathvariant="normal">ℜ<!-- ℜ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>s</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>&lt;</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle 0&lt;\Re (s)&lt;{\frac {1}{2}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="0 &lt; \Re(s) &lt; \frac12." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6be3c2ab0ab9da4312ccfdbe7301a5af3c07f8a4" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:14.923ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>That ζ has only simple zeros on the critical line is equivalent to its derivative having no zeros on the critical line.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consequences_of_the_generalized_Riemann_hypothesis">Consequences of the generalized Riemann hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Consequences of the generalized Riemann hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several applications use the <a href="/wiki/Generalized_Riemann_hypothesis" title="Generalized Riemann hypothesis">generalized Riemann hypothesis</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dirichlet_L-series" title="Dirichlet L-series">Dirichlet L-series</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dedekind_zeta_function" title="Dedekind zeta function">zeta functions of number fields</a> rather than just the Riemann hypothesis. Many basic properties of the Riemann zeta function can easily be generalized to all Dirichlet L-series, so it is plausible that a method that proves the Riemann hypothesis for the Riemann zeta function would also work for the generalized Riemann hypothesis for Dirichlet L-functions. Several results first proved using the generalized Riemann hypothesis were later given unconditional proofs without using it, though these were usually much harder. Many of the consequences on the following list are taken from <a href="#CITEREFConrad2010">Conrad (2010)</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li>In 1913, Gronwall showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis implies that Gauss's <a href="/wiki/Class_number_problem" title="Class number problem">list of imaginary quadratic fields with class number 1</a> is complete, though Baker, Stark and Heegner later gave unconditional proofs of this without using the generalized Riemann hypothesis.</li>
<li>In 1917, Hardy and Littlewood showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis implies a conjecture of Chebyshev that</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<munder>
<mo form="prefix" movablelimits="true">lim</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
<msup>
<mn>1</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mrow>
</munder>
<munder>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>p</mi>
<mo>&gt;</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</munder>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mn>1</mn>
<msup>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>

<mn>1</mn>

<mo>/</mo>
</mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
</mrow>
</msup>

<mi>x</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>p</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo>=</mo>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi mathvariant="normal">∞<!-- ∞ --></mi>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lim _{x\to 1^{-}}\sum _{p&gt;2}(-1)^{(p+1)/2}x^{p}=+\infty ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lim_{x\to 1^-}\sum_{p&gt;2}(-1)^{(p+1)/2}x^p=+\infty," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f985ea88bc9a21e500bd4b0c5cbd26ef95bd6d" style="vertical-align: -3.338ex; width:29.67ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
<dd>which says that in some sense primes 3 mod 4 are more common than primes 1 mod 4.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>In 1923 Hardy and Littlewood showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis implies a weak form of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goldbach_conjecture" title="Goldbach conjecture">Goldbach conjecture</a> for odd numbers: that every sufficiently large odd number is the sum of three primes, though in 1937 Vinogradov gave an unconditional proof. In 1997 <a href="/wiki/Jean-Marc_Deshouillers" title="Jean-Marc Deshouillers">Deshouillers</a>, Effinger, te Riele, and Zinoviev showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis implies that every odd number greater than 5 is the sum of three primes.</li>
<li>In 1934, Chowla showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis implies that the first prime in the arithmetic progression <i>a</i> mod <i>m</i> is at most <i>Km</i><sup>2</sup>log(<i>m</i>)<sup>2</sup> for some fixed constant <i>K</i>.</li>
<li>In 1967, Hooley showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis implies <a href="/wiki/Artin%27s_conjecture_on_primitive_roots" title="Artin's conjecture on primitive roots">Artin's conjecture on primitive roots</a>.</li>
<li>In 1973, Weinberger showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis implies that Euler's list of <a href="/wiki/Idoneal_number" title="Idoneal number">idoneal numbers</a> is complete.</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFWeinberger1973">Weinberger (1973)</a> showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis for the zeta functions of all algebraic number fields implies that any number field with class number 1 is either <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_domain" title="Euclidean domain">Euclidean</a> or an imaginary quadratic number field of discriminant −19, −43, −67, or −163.</li>
<li>In 1976, G. Miller showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis implies that one can <a href="/wiki/Primality_test" title="Primality test">test if a number is prime</a> in polynomial time via the <a href="/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Rabin_primality_test" title="Miller–Rabin primality test">Miller test</a>. In 2002, Manindra Agrawal, Neeraj Kayal and Nitin Saxena proved this result unconditionally using the <a href="/wiki/AKS_primality_test" title="AKS primality test">AKS primality test</a>.</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFOdlyzko1990">Odlyzko (1990)</a> discussed how the generalized Riemann hypothesis can be used to give sharper estimates for discriminants and class numbers of number fields.</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFOnoSoundararajan1997">Ono &amp; Soundararajan (1997)</a> showed that the generalized Riemann hypothesis implies that <a href="/wiki/Ramanujan%27s_ternary_quadratic_form" title="Ramanujan's ternary quadratic form">Ramanujan's integral quadratic form</a> <i>x</i><sup>2</sup> +<i>y</i><sup>2</sup> + 10<i>z</i><sup>2</sup> represents all integers that it represents locally, with exactly 18 exceptions.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Excluded_middle">Excluded middle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Excluded middle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some consequences of the RH are also consequences of its negation, and are thus theorems. In their discussion of the <a href="#Gauss.27s_class_number_conjecture">Hecke, Deuring, Mordell, Heilbronn theorem</a>, (<a href="#CITEREFIrelandRosen1990">Ireland &amp; Rosen 1990</a>, p. 359) say</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The method of proof here is truly amazing. If the generalized Riemann hypothesis is true, then the theorem is true. If the generalized Riemann hypothesis is false, then the theorem is true. Thus, the theorem is true!!     (punctuation in original)</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Care should be taken to understand what is meant by saying the generalized Riemann hypothesis is false: one should specify exactly which class of Dirichlet series has a counterexample.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Littlewood.27s_theorem">Littlewood's theorem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Littlewood's theorem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This concerns the sign of the error in the <a href="/wiki/Prime_number_theorem" title="Prime number theorem">prime number theorem</a>. It has been computed that π(<i>x</i>) &lt; Li(<i>x</i>) for all <i>x</i> ≤ 10<sup>23</sup>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and no value of <i>x</i> is known for which π(<i>x</i>) &gt; Li(<i>x</i>). See this <a href="/wiki/Prime_number_theorem#Table_of_.CF.80.28x.29.2C_x_.2F_ln_x.2C_and_li.28x.29" title="Prime number theorem">table</a>.</p>
<p>In 1914 Littlewood proved that there are arbitrarily large values of <i>x</i> for which</p>

<p>Thus the difference π(<i>x</i>) − Li(<i>x</i>) changes sign infinitely many times. <a href="/wiki/Skewes%27_number" title="Skewes' number">Skewes' number</a> is an estimate of the value of <i>x</i> corresponding to the first sign change.</p>
<p>Littlewood's proof is divided into two cases: the RH is assumed false (about half a page of <a href="#CITEREFIngham1932">Ingham 1932</a>, Chapt. V), and the RH is assumed true (about a dozen pages).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gauss.27s_class_number_conjecture">Gauss's class number conjecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Gauss's class number conjecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>This is the <a href="/wiki/Class_number_problem" title="Class number problem">conjecture</a> (first stated in article 303 of Gauss's <a href="/wiki/Disquisitiones_Arithmeticae" title="Disquisitiones Arithmeticae">Disquisitiones Arithmeticae</a>) that there are only a finite number of imaginary quadratic fields with a given class number. One way to prove it would be to show that as the discriminant <i>D</i> → −∞ the class number <i>h</i>(<i>D</i>) → ∞.</p>
<p>The following sequence of theorems involving the Riemann hypothesis is described in <a href="#CITEREFIrelandRosen1990">Ireland &amp; Rosen 1990</a>, pp. 358–361:</p>
<blockquote>
<p><b>Theorem (Hecke; 1918).</b> Let <i>D</i> &lt; 0 be the discriminant of an imaginary quadratic number field <i>K</i>. Assume the generalized Riemann hypothesis for <i>L</i>-functions of all imaginary quadratic Dirichlet characters. Then there is an absolute constant <i>C</i> such that</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>h</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>D</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>&gt;</mo>
<mi>C</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<msqrt>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>D</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
</msqrt>
<mrow>
<mi>log</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>D</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle h(D)&gt;C{\frac {\sqrt {|D|}}{\log |D|}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="h(D) &gt; C\frac{\sqrt{|D|}}{\log |D|}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d952a15867da8e64956e8a2172ae738ea7d70131" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:18.133ex; height:7.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p><b>Theorem (Deuring; 1933).</b> If the RH is false then <i>h</i>(<i>D</i>) &gt; 1 if |<i>D</i>| is sufficiently large.</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p><b>Theorem (Mordell; 1934).</b> If the RH is false then <i>h</i>(<i>D</i>) → ∞ as <i>D</i> → −∞.</p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote>
<p><b>Theorem (Heilbronn; 1934).</b> If the generalized RH is false for the <i>L</i>-function of some imaginary quadratic Dirichlet character then <i>h</i>(<i>D</i>) → ∞ as <i>D</i> → −∞.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>(In the work of Hecke and Heilbronn, the only <i>L</i>-functions that occur are those attached to imaginary quadratic characters, and it is only for those <i>L</i>-functions that <i>GRH is true</i> or <i>GRH is false</i> is intended; a failure of GRH for the <i>L</i>-function of a cubic Dirichlet character would, strictly speaking, mean GRH is false, but that was not the kind of failure of GRH that Heilbronn had in mind, so his assumption was more restricted than simply <i>GRH is false</i>.)</p>
<p>In 1935, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Siegel" title="Carl Siegel">Carl Siegel</a> later strengthened the result without using RH or GRH in any way.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_of_Euler.27s_totient">Growth of Euler's totient</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Growth of Euler's totient">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1983 <a href="/wiki/Jean-Louis_Nicolas" title="Jean-Louis Nicolas">J. L. Nicolas</a> proved (<a href="#CITEREFRibenboim1996">Ribenboim 1996</a>, p. 320) that</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>φ<!-- φ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>&lt;</mo>
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>γ<!-- γ --></mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow>
<mi>log</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>log</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mi>n</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \varphi (n)&lt;e^{-\gamma }{\frac {n}{\log \log n}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\varphi(n) &lt; e^{-\gamma}\frac  {n} {\log \log n} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/98953eb8832acb6f3cf92c1ef1d18da322c3cd77" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:20.409ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>for infinitely many <i>n</i>, where φ(<i>n</i>) is <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_totient_function" title="Euler's totient function">Euler's totient function</a> and γ is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler%27s_constant" title="Euler's constant">Euler's constant</a>.</p>
<p>Ribenboim remarks that:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The method of proof is interesting, in that the inequality is shown first under the assumption that the Riemann hypothesis is true, secondly under the contrary assumption.</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalizations_and_analogs">Generalizations and analogs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Generalizations and analogs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dirichlet_L-series_and_other_number_fields">Dirichlet L-series and other number fields</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dirichlet L-series and other number fields">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Riemann hypothesis can be generalized by replacing the Riemann zeta function by the formally similar, but much more general, global <a href="/wiki/L-function" title="L-function">L-functions</a>. In this broader setting, one expects the non-trivial zeros of the global <i>L</i>-functions to have real part 1/2. It is these conjectures, rather than the classical Riemann hypothesis only for the single Riemann zeta function, which account for the true importance of the Riemann hypothesis in mathematics.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Generalized_Riemann_hypothesis" title="Generalized Riemann hypothesis">generalized Riemann hypothesis</a> extends the Riemann hypothesis to all <a href="/wiki/Dirichlet_L-function" title="Dirichlet L-function">Dirichlet L-functions</a>. In particular it implies the conjecture that <a href="/wiki/Siegel_zero" title="Siegel zero">Siegel zeros</a> (zeros of <i>L</i>-functions between 1/2 and 1) do not exist.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extended_Riemann_hypothesis" title="Extended Riemann hypothesis">extended Riemann hypothesis</a> extends the Riemann hypothesis to all <a href="/wiki/Dedekind_zeta_function" title="Dedekind zeta function">Dedekind zeta functions</a> of <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_number_field" title="Algebraic number field">algebraic number fields</a>. The extended Riemann hypothesis for abelian extension of the rationals is equivalent to the generalized Riemann hypothesis. The Riemann hypothesis can also be extended to the <i>L</i>-functions of <a href="/wiki/Hecke_character" title="Hecke character">Hecke characters</a> of number fields.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Riemann_hypothesis" title="Grand Riemann hypothesis">grand Riemann hypothesis</a> extends it to all <a href="/wiki/Automorphic_L-function" title="Automorphic L-function">automorphic zeta functions</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Mellin_transform" title="Mellin transform">Mellin transforms</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hecke_eigenform" title="Hecke eigenform">Hecke eigenforms</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Function_fields_and_zeta_functions_of_varieties_over_finite_fields">Function fields and zeta functions of varieties over finite fields</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Function fields and zeta functions of varieties over finite fields">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="#CITEREFArtin1924">Artin (1924)</a> introduced global zeta functions of (quadratic) <a href="/wiki/Function_field_of_an_algebraic_variety" title="Function field of an algebraic variety">function fields</a> and conjectured an analogue of the Riemann hypothesis for them, which has been proved by Hasse in the genus 1 case and by <a href="#CITEREFWeil1948">Weil (1948)</a> in general. For instance, the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Quadratic_Gauss_sum" title="Quadratic Gauss sum">Gauss sum</a>, of the quadratic character of a <a href="/wiki/Finite_field" title="Finite field">finite field</a> of size <i>q</i> (with <i>q</i> odd), has absolute value <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mi>q</mi>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\sqrt {q}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\sqrt{q}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fec68a26ee361e6e7450c74fadea8334650ec369" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:3.026ex; height:3.009ex;"/></span> is actually an instance of the Riemann hypothesis in the function field setting. This led <a href="#CITEREFWeil1949">Weil (1949)</a> to conjecture a similar statement for all <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_variety" title="Algebraic variety">algebraic varieties</a>; the resulting <a href="/wiki/Weil_conjectures" title="Weil conjectures">Weil conjectures</a> were proved by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Deligne" title="Pierre Deligne">Pierre Deligne</a> (<a href="#CITEREFDeligne1974">1974</a>, <a href="#CITEREFDeligne1980">1980</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arithmetic_zeta_functions_of_arithmetic_schemes_and_their_L-factors">Arithmetic zeta functions of arithmetic schemes and their L-factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Arithmetic zeta functions of arithmetic schemes and their L-factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_zeta_function" title="Arithmetic zeta function">Arithmetic zeta functions</a> generalise the Riemann and Dedekind zeta functions as well as the zeta functions of varieties over finite fields to every arithmetic scheme or a scheme of finite type over integers. The arithmetic zeta function of a regular connected <a href="/wiki/Equidimensionality" title="Equidimensionality">equidimensional</a> arithmetic scheme of Kronecker dimension <i>n</i> can be factorized into the product of appropriately defined L-factors and an auxiliary factor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.-P._Serre" title="J.-P. Serre">Jean-Pierre Serre</a> (<a href="#CITEREFSerre1969.E2.80.931970">1969–1970</a>). Assuming a functional equation and meromorphic continuation, the generalized Riemann hypothesis for the L-factor states that its zeros inside the critical strip <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">ℜ<!-- ℜ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>s</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>∈<!-- ∈ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Re (s)\in (0,n)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Re(s)\in (0,n)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d17074a0a626149034199b2dd2346d45afc300b9" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:13.17ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> lie on the central line. Correspondingly, the generalized Riemann hypothesis for the arithmetic zeta function of a regular connected equidimensional arithmetic scheme states that its zeros inside the critical strip lie on vertical lines <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>

This is known for schemes in positive characteristic and follows from <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Deligne" title="Pierre Deligne">Pierre Deligne</a> (<a href="#CITEREFDeligne1974">1974</a>, <a href="#CITEREFDeligne1980">1980</a>), but remains entirely unknown in characteristic zero.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Selberg_zeta_functions">Selberg zeta functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Selberg zeta functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Selberg_zeta_function" title="Selberg zeta function">Selberg zeta function</a></div>
<p><a href="#CITEREFSelberg1956">Selberg (1956)</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Selberg_zeta_function" title="Selberg zeta function">Selberg zeta function</a> of a Riemann surface. These are similar to the Riemann zeta function: they have a functional equation, and an infinite product similar to the Euler product but taken over closed geodesics rather than primes. The <a href="/wiki/Selberg_trace_formula" title="Selberg trace formula">Selberg trace formula</a> is the analogue for these functions of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Explicit_formula_(L-function)" title="Explicit formula (L-function)">explicit formulas</a> in prime number theory. Selberg proved that the Selberg zeta functions satisfy the analogue of the Riemann hypothesis, with the imaginary parts of their zeros related to the eigenvalues of the Laplacian operator of the Riemann surface.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ihara_zeta_functions">Ihara zeta functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Ihara zeta functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ihara_zeta_function" title="Ihara zeta function">Ihara zeta function</a> of a finite graph is an analogue of the <a href="/wiki/Selberg_zeta_function" title="Selberg zeta function">Selberg zeta function</a>, which was first introduced by <a href="/wiki/Yasutaka_Ihara" title="Yasutaka Ihara">Yasutaka Ihara</a> in the context of discrete subgroups of the two-by-two p-adic special linear group. A regular finite graph is a <a href="/wiki/Ramanujan_graph" title="Ramanujan graph">Ramanujan graph</a>, a mathematical model of efficient communication networks, if and only if its Ihara zeta function satisfies the analogue of the Riemann hypothesis as was pointed out by <a href="/wiki/Toshikazu_Sunada" title="Toshikazu Sunada">T. Sunada</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Montgomery.27s_pair_correlation_conjecture">Montgomery's pair correlation conjecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Montgomery's pair correlation conjecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="#CITEREFMontgomery1973">Montgomery (1973)</a> suggested the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pair_correlation_conjecture" title="Pair correlation conjecture">pair correlation conjecture</a> that the correlation functions of the (suitably normalized) zeros of the zeta function should be the same as those of the eigenvalues of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaussian_unitary_ensemble" title="Gaussian unitary ensemble">random hermitian matrix</a>. <a href="#CITEREFOdlyzko1987">Odlyzko (1987)</a> showed that this is supported by large scale numerical calculations of these correlation functions.</p>
<p>Montgomery showed that (assuming the Riemann hypothesis) at least 2/3 of all zeros are simple, and a related conjecture is that all zeros of the zeta function are simple (or more generally have no non-trivial integer linear relations between their imaginary parts). <a href="/wiki/Dedekind_zeta_function" title="Dedekind zeta function">Dedekind zeta functions</a> of algebraic number fields, which generalize the Riemann zeta function, often do have multiple complex zeros (<a href="#CITEREFRadziejewski2007">Radziejewski 2007</a>). This is because the Dedekind zeta functions factorize as a product of powers of <a href="/wiki/Artin_L-function" title="Artin L-function">Artin L-functions</a>, so zeros of Artin L-functions sometimes give rise to multiple zeros of Dedekind zeta functions. Other examples of zeta functions with multiple zeros are the L-functions of some <a href="/wiki/Elliptic_curve" title="Elliptic curve">elliptic curves</a>: these can have multiple zeros at the real point of their critical line; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birch-Swinnerton-Dyer_conjecture" title="Birch-Swinnerton-Dyer conjecture">Birch-Swinnerton-Dyer conjecture</a> predicts that the multiplicity of this zero is the rank of the elliptic curve.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_zeta_functions">Other zeta functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other zeta functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeta_function" title="Zeta function">many other examples</a> of zeta functions with analogues of the Riemann hypothesis, some of which have been proved. <a href="/wiki/Goss_zeta_function" title="Goss zeta function">Goss zeta functions</a> of function fields have a Riemann hypothesis, proved by <a href="#CITEREFSheats1998">Sheats (1998)</a>. <a href="/wiki/Main_conjecture_of_Iwasawa_theory" title="Main conjecture of Iwasawa theory">The main conjecture</a> of <a href="/wiki/Iwasawa_theory" title="Iwasawa theory">Iwasawa theory</a>, proved by <a href="/wiki/Barry_Mazur" title="Barry Mazur">Barry Mazur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Wiles" title="Andrew Wiles">Andrew Wiles</a> for <a href="/wiki/Cyclotomic_field" title="Cyclotomic field">cyclotomic fields</a>, and Wiles for <a href="/wiki/Totally_real_number_field" title="Totally real number field">totally real fields</a>, identifies the zeros of a <i>p</i>-adic <i>L</i>-function with the eigenvalues of an operator, so can be thought of as an analogue of the <a href="/wiki/Hilbert%E2%80%93P%C3%B3lya_conjecture" title="Hilbert–Pólya conjecture">Hilbert–Pólya conjecture</a> for <a href="/wiki/P-adic_L-function" title="P-adic L-function"><i>p</i>-adic <i>L</i>-functions</a> (<a href="#CITEREFWiles2000">Wiles 2000</a>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempted_proofs">Attempted proofs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Attempted proofs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Several mathematicians have addressed the Riemann hypothesis, but none of their attempts have yet been accepted as correct solutions. <a href="#CITEREFWatkins2007">Watkins (2007)</a> lists some incorrect solutions, and more are <a class="external text" href="http://arxiv.org/find/grp_math/1/AND+ti:+AND+Riemann+hypothesis+subj:+AND+General+mathematics/0/1/0/all/0/1" rel="nofollow">frequently announced</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operator_theory">Operator theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Operator theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hilbert%E2%80%93P%C3%B3lya_conjecture" title="Hilbert–Pólya conjecture">Hilbert–Pólya conjecture</a></div>
<p>Hilbert and Pólya suggested that one way to derive the Riemann hypothesis would be to find a <a href="/wiki/Self-adjoint_operator" title="Self-adjoint operator">self-adjoint operator</a>, from the existence of which the statement on the real parts of the zeros of ζ(<i>s</i>) would follow when one applies the criterion on real <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eigenvalue" title="Eigenvalue">eigenvalues</a>. Some support for this idea comes from several analogues of the Riemann zeta functions whose zeros correspond to eigenvalues of some operator: the zeros of a zeta function of a variety over a finite field correspond to eigenvalues of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frobenius_element" title="Frobenius element">Frobenius element</a> on an <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tale_cohomology" title="Étale cohomology">étale cohomology</a> group, the zeros of a <a href="/wiki/Selberg_zeta_function" title="Selberg zeta function">Selberg zeta function</a> are eigenvalues of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laplacian_operator" title="Laplacian operator">Laplacian operator</a> of a Riemann surface, and the zeros of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-adic_zeta_function" title="P-adic zeta function">p-adic zeta function</a> correspond to eigenvectors of a Galois action on <a href="/wiki/Ideal_class_group" title="Ideal class group">ideal class groups</a>.</p>
<p><a href="#CITEREFOdlyzko1987">Odlyzko (1987)</a> showed that the distribution of the zeros of the Riemann zeta function shares some statistical properties with the eigenvalues of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Random_matrices" title="Random matrices">random matrices</a> drawn from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaussian_unitary_ensemble" title="Gaussian unitary ensemble">Gaussian unitary ensemble</a>. This gives some support to the Hilbert–Pólya conjecture.</p>
<p>In 1999, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Berry_(physicist)" title="Michael Berry (physicist)">Michael Berry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Keating" title="Jonathan Keating">Jonathan Keating</a> conjectured that there is some unknown quantization <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mo stretchy="false">^<!-- ^ --></mo>
</mover>

<p>and even more strongly, that the Riemann zeros coincide with the spectrum of the operator <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

This is in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Canonical_quantization" title="Canonical quantization">canonical quantization</a>, which leads to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heisenberg_uncertainty_principle" title="Heisenberg uncertainty principle">Heisenberg uncertainty principle</a> <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

In a connection with this quantum mechanical problem Berry and Connes had proposed that the inverse of the potential of the Hamiltonian is connected to the half-derivative of the function</p>

This yields to a Hamiltonian whose eigenvalues are the square of the imaginary part of the Riemann zeros, and also the functional determinant of this Hamiltonian operator is just the <a href="/wiki/Riemann_Xi_function" title="Riemann Xi function">Riemann Xi function</a>. In fact the Riemann Xi function would be proportional to the functional determinant (Hadamard product)</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</dl>
<p>as proved by Connes and others, in this approach</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
