</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\frac {\xi (s)}{\xi (0)}}={\frac {\det(H+s(s-1)+1/4)}{\det(H+1/4)}}.}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt=" \frac{\xi(s)}{\xi(0)}=\frac{\det(H+s(s-1)+1/4)}{\det(H+1/4)}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b889a21be626801a95dbf32502a6706ca45ce65e" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:33.923ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The analogy with the Riemann hypothesis over finite fields suggests that the Hilbert space containing eigenvectors corresponding to the zeros might be some sort of first cohomology group of the <a href="/wiki/Spectrum_of_a_ring" title="Spectrum of a ring">spectrum</a> Spec(<b>Z</b>) of the integers. <a href="#CITEREFDeninger1998">Deninger (1998)</a> described some of the attempts to find such a cohomology theory (<a href="#CITEREFLeichtnam2005">Leichtnam 2005</a>).</p>
<p><a href="#CITEREFZagier1981">Zagier (1981)</a> constructed a natural space of invariant functions on the upper half plane that has eigenvalues under the Laplacian operator that correspond to zeros of the Riemann zeta function—and remarked that in the unlikely event that one could show the existence of a suitable positive definite inner product on this space, the Riemann hypothesis would follow. <a href="#CITEREFCartier1982">Cartier (1982)</a> discussed a related example, where due to a bizarre bug a computer program listed zeros of the Riemann zeta function as eigenvalues of the same Laplacian operator.</p>
<p><a href="#CITEREFSchumayerHutchinson2011">Schumayer &amp; Hutchinson (2011)</a> surveyed some of the attempts to construct a suitable physical model related to the Riemann zeta function.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lee.E2.80.93Yang_theorem">Lee–Yang theorem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Lee–Yang theorem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lee%E2%80%93Yang_theorem" title="Lee–Yang theorem">Lee–Yang theorem</a> states that the zeros of certain partition functions in statistical mechanics all lie on a "critical line" with real part 0, and this has led to some speculation about a relationship with the Riemann hypothesis (<a href="#CITEREFKnauf1999">Knauf 1999</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (December 2013)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tur.C3.A1n.27s_result">Turán's result</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Turán's result">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1l_Tur%C3%A1n" title="Pál Turán">Pál Turán</a> (<a href="#CITEREFTur.C3.A1n1948">1948</a>) showed that if the functions</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<munderover>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>N</mi>
</mrow>
</munderover>
<msup>
<mi>n</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>s</mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \sum _{n=1}^{N}n^{-s}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\sum_{n=1}^N n^{-s}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/80313e1f98aa8dcaf8b2543713ea1a6f8948c7f7" style="vertical-align: -3.005ex; width:7.454ex; height:7.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>have no zeros when the real part of <i>s</i> is greater than one then</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<munder>
<mo>∑<!-- ∑ --></mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>n</mi>
<mo>≤<!-- ≤ --></mo>
<mi>x</mi>
</mrow>
</munder>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>n</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
<mi>n</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>≥<!-- ≥ --></mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mtext> for </mtext>
</mrow>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>&gt;</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T(x)=\sum _{n\leq x}{\frac {\lambda (n)}{n}}\geq 0{\text{ for }}x&gt;0,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="T(x) = \sum_{n\le x}\frac{\lambda(n)}{n}\ge 0\text{ for } x &gt; 0," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d19e84b13ee72081ccf1b776c455a4f635150270" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:31.655ex; height:7.009ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where λ(<i>n</i>) is the <a href="/wiki/Liouville_function" title="Liouville function">Liouville function</a> given by (−1)<sup><i>r</i></sup> if <i>n</i> has <i>r</i> prime factors. He showed that this in turn would imply that the Riemann hypothesis is true. However <a href="#CITEREFHaselgrove1958">Haselgrove (1958)</a> proved that <i>T</i>(<i>x</i>) is negative for infinitely many <i>x</i> (and also disproved the closely related <a href="/wiki/P%C3%B3lya_conjecture" title="Pólya conjecture">Pólya conjecture</a>), and <a href="#CITEREFBorweinFergusonMossinghoff2008">Borwein, Ferguson &amp; Mossinghoff (2008)</a> showed that the smallest such <i>x</i> is <span style="white-space:nowrap">72<span style="margin-left:0.25em">185</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em">376</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em">951</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em">205</span></span>. <a href="#CITEREFSpira1968">Spira (1968)</a> showed by numerical calculation that the finite Dirichlet series above for <i>N</i>=19 has a zero with real part greater than 1. Turán also showed that a somewhat weaker assumption, the nonexistence of zeros with real part greater than 1+<i>N</i><sup>−1/2+ε</sup> for large <i>N</i> in the finite Dirichlet series above, would also imply the Riemann hypothesis, but <a href="#CITEREFMontgomery1983">Montgomery (1983)</a> showed that for all sufficiently large <i>N</i> these series have zeros with real part greater than <span class="nowrap">1 + (log log <i>N</i>)/(4 log <i>N</i>)</span>. Therefore, Turán's result is <a href="/wiki/Vacuous_truth" title="Vacuous truth">vacuously true</a> and cannot be used to help prove the Riemann hypothesis.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noncommutative_geometry">Noncommutative geometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Noncommutative geometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alain_Connes" title="Alain Connes">Connes</a> (<a href="#CITEREFConnes1999">1999</a>, <a href="#CITEREFConnes2000">2000</a>) has described a relationship between the Riemann hypothesis and <a href="/wiki/Noncommutative_geometry" title="Noncommutative geometry">noncommutative geometry</a>, and shows that a suitable analog of the <a href="/wiki/Selberg_trace_formula" title="Selberg trace formula">Selberg trace formula</a> for the action of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Id%C3%A8le_class_group" title="Idèle class group">idèle class group</a> on the adèle class space would imply the Riemann hypothesis. Some of these ideas are elaborated in <a href="#CITEREFLapidus2008">Lapidus (2008)</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hilbert_spaces_of_entire_functions">Hilbert spaces of entire functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Hilbert spaces of entire functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_de_Branges" title="Louis de Branges">Louis de Branges</a> (<a href="#CITEREFde_Branges1992">1992</a>) showed that the Riemann hypothesis would follow from a positivity condition on a certain Hilbert space of <a href="/wiki/Entire_function" title="Entire function">entire functions</a>. However <a href="#CITEREFConreyLi2000">Conrey &amp; Li (2000)</a> showed that the necessary positivity conditions are not satisfied. Despite this obstacle, de Branges has continued to work on an attempted proof of the Riemann hypothesis along the same lines, but this has not been widely accepted by other mathematicians (<a href="#CITEREFSarnak2005">Sarnak 2005</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasicrystals">Quasicrystals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Quasicrystals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Riemann hypothesis implies that the zeros of the zeta function form a <a href="/wiki/Quasicrystal" title="Quasicrystal">quasicrystal</a>, meaning a distribution with discrete support whose Fourier transform also has discrete support. <a href="#CITEREFDyson2009">Dyson (2009)</a> suggested trying to prove the Riemann hypothesis by classifying, or at least studying, 1-dimensional quasicrystals.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arithmetic_zeta_functions_of_models_of_elliptic_curves_over_number_fields">Arithmetic zeta functions of models of elliptic curves over number fields</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Arithmetic zeta functions of models of elliptic curves over number fields">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When one goes from geometric dimension one, e.g. an algebraic number field, to geometric dimension two, e.g. a regular model of an <a href="/wiki/Elliptic_curve" title="Elliptic curve">elliptic curve</a> over a number field, the two-dimensional part of the generalized Riemann hypothesis for the <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic_zeta_function" title="Arithmetic zeta function">arithmetic zeta function</a> of the model deals with the poles of the zeta function. In dimension one the study of the zeta integral in <a href="/wiki/Tate%27s_thesis" title="Tate's thesis">Tate's thesis</a> does not lead to new important information on the Riemann hypothesis. Contrary to this, in dimension two work of <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Fesenko" title="Ivan Fesenko">Ivan Fesenko</a> on two-dimensional generalisation of Tate's thesis includes an integral representation of a zeta integral closely related to the zeta function. In this new situation, not possible in dimension one, the poles of the zeta function can be studied via the zeta integral and associated adele groups. Related conjecture of <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Fesenko" title="Ivan Fesenko">Fesenko</a> (<a href="#CITEREFFesenko2010">2010</a>) on the positivity of the fourth derivative of a boundary function associated to the zeta integral essentially implies the pole part of the generalized Riemann hypothesis. Suzuki (<a href="#CITEREFSuzuki2011">2011</a>) proved that the latter, together with some technical assumptions, implies Fesenko's conjecture.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multiple_zeta_functions">Multiple zeta functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Multiple zeta functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Deligne's proof of the Riemann hypothesis over finite fields used the zeta functions of product varieties, whose zeros and poles correspond to sums of zeros and poles of the original zeta function, in order to bound the real parts of the zeros of the original zeta function. By analogy, <a href="#CITEREFKurokawa1992">Kurokawa (1992)</a> introduced multiple zeta functions whose zeros and poles correspond to sums of zeros and poles of the Riemann zeta function. To make the series converge he restricted to sums of zeros or poles all with non-negative imaginary part. So far, the known bounds on the zeros and poles of the multiple zeta functions are not strong enough to give useful estimates for the zeros of the Riemann zeta function.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Location_of_the_zeros">Location of the zeros</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Location of the zeros">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>and a small but rather mysterious term</p>

</dl>
<p>So the density of zeros with imaginary part near <i>T</i> is about log(<i>T</i>)/2π, and the function <i>S</i> describes the small deviations from this. The function <i>S</i>(<i>t</i>) jumps by 1 at each zero of the zeta function, and for <span class="nowrap"><i>t</i> ≥ 8</span> it decreases monotonically between zeros with derivative close to −log <i>t</i>.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatolii_Alexeevitch_Karatsuba" title="Anatolii Alexeevitch Karatsuba">Karatsuba</a> (1996) proved that every interval (<i>T</i>, <i>T</i>+<i>H</i>] for <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>points where the function <i>S</i>(<i>t</i>) changes sign.</p>
<p><a href="#CITEREFSelberg1946">Selberg (1946)</a> showed that the average moments of even powers of <i>S</i> are given by</p>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \int _{0}^{T}|S(t)|^{2k}dt={\frac {(2k)!}{k!(2\pi )^{2k}}}T(\log \log T)^{k}+O(T(\log \log T)^{k-1/2}).}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\int_0^T|S(t)|^{2k}dt = \frac{(2k)!}{k!(2\pi)^{2k}}T(\log \log T)^k + O(T(\log \log T)^{k-1/2})." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe755065e201b43888916edee42e9e9f5409b0fe" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:62.756ex; height:6.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>This suggests that <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>)/(log log <i>T</i>)<sup>1/2</sup> resembles a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaussian_random_variable" title="Gaussian random variable">Gaussian random variable</a> with mean 0 and variance 2π<sup>2</sup> (<a href="#CITEREFGhosh1983">Ghosh (1983)</a> proved this fact). In particular |<i>S</i>(<i>T</i>)| is usually somewhere around (log log <i>T</i>)<sup>1/2</sup>, but occasionally much larger. The exact order of growth of <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>) is not known. There has been no unconditional improvement to Riemann's original bound <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>)=O(log <i>T</i>), though the Riemann hypothesis implies the slightly smaller bound <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>)=O(log <i>T</i>/log log <i>T</i>) (<a href="#CITEREFTitchmarsh1986">Titchmarsh 1986</a>). The true order of magnitude may be somewhat less than this, as random functions with the same distribution as <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>) tend to have growth of order about log(<i>T</i>)<sup>1/2</sup>. In the other direction it cannot be too small: <a href="#CITEREFSelberg1946">Selberg (1946)</a> showed that <span class="nowrap"><i>S</i>(<i>T</i>) ≠ o((log <i>T</i>)<sup>1/3</sup>/(log log <i>T</i>)<sup>7/3</sup>)</span>, and assuming the Riemann hypothesis Montgomery showed that <span class="nowrap"><i>S</i>(<i>T</i>) ≠ o((log <i>T</i>)<sup>1/2</sup>/(log log <i>T</i>)<sup>1/2</sup>)</span>.</p>
<p>Numerical calculations confirm that <i>S</i> grows very slowly: |<i>S</i>(<i>T</i>)| &lt; 1 for <span class="nowrap"><i>T</i> &lt; 280</span>, |<i>S</i>(<i>T</i>)| &lt; 2 for <i>T</i> &lt; <span style="white-space:nowrap">6<span style="margin-left:0.25em">800</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em">000</span></span>, and the largest value of |<i>S</i>(<i>T</i>)| found so far is not much larger than 3 (<a href="#CITEREFOdlyzko2002">Odlyzko 2002</a>).</p>
<p>Riemann's estimate <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>) = O(log <i>T</i>) implies that the gaps between zeros are bounded, and Littlewood improved this slightly, showing that the gaps between their imaginary parts tends to 0.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theorem_of_Hadamard_and_de_la_Vall.C3.A9e-Poussin">Theorem of Hadamard and de la Vallée-Poussin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Theorem of Hadamard and de la Vallée-Poussin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="#CITEREFHadamard1896">Hadamard (1896)</a> and <a href="#CITEREFde_la_Vall.C3.A9e-Poussin1896">de la Vallée-Poussin (1896)</a> independently proved that no zeros could lie on the line Re(<i>s</i>) = 1. Together with the functional equation and the fact that there are no zeros with real part greater than 1, this showed that all non-trivial zeros must lie in the interior of the critical strip <span class="nowrap">0 &lt; Re(<i>s</i>) &lt; 1</span>. This was a key step in their first proofs of the <a href="/wiki/Prime_number_theorem" title="Prime number theorem">prime number theorem</a>.</p>
<p>Both the original proofs that the zeta function has no zeros with real part 1 are similar, and depend on showing that if ζ(1+<i>it</i>) vanishes, then ζ(1+2<i>it</i>) is singular, which is not possible. One way of doing this is by using the inequality</p>
<dl>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zero-free_regions">Zero-free regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Zero-free regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="#CITEREFde_la_Vall.C3.A9e-Poussin1899.E2.80.931900">De la Vallée-Poussin (1899–1900)</a> proved that if <span class="nowrap">σ + <i>i<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap"> </span>t</i></span> is a zero of the Riemann zeta function, then <span class="nowrap">1 − σ ≥ <big><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><i>C</i></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">log(<i>t</i>)</span></span></big></span> for some positive constant <i>C</i>. In other words, zeros cannot be too close to the line <span class="nowrap">σ = 1:</span> there is a zero-free region close to this line. This zero-free region has been enlarged by several authors using methods such as <a href="/wiki/Vinogradov%27s_mean-value_theorem" title="Vinogradov's mean-value theorem">Vinogradov's mean-value theorem</a>. <a href="#CITEREFFord2002">Ford (2002)</a> gave a version with explicit numerical constants: <span class="nowrap">ζ(σ + <i>i<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:nowrap"> </span>t</i> ) ≠ 0</span> whenever <span class="nowrap">|<i>t</i> | ≥ 3</span> and</p>

<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Zeros_on_the_critical_line">Zeros on the critical line</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Zeros on the critical line">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="#CITEREFHardy1914">Hardy (1914)</a> and <a href="#CITEREFHardyLittlewood1921">Hardy &amp; Littlewood (1921)</a> showed there are infinitely many zeros on the critical line, by considering moments of certain functions related to the zeta function. <a href="#CITEREFSelberg1942">Selberg (1942)</a> proved that at least a (small) positive proportion of zeros lie on the line. <a href="#CITEREFLevinson1974">Levinson (1974)</a> improved this to one-third of the zeros by relating the zeros of the zeta function to those of its derivative, and <a href="#CITEREFConrey1989">Conrey (1989)</a> improved this further to two-fifths.</p>
<p>Most zeros lie close to the critical line. More precisely, <a href="#CITEREFBohrLandau1914">Bohr &amp; Landau (1914)</a> showed that for any positive ε, all but an infinitely small proportion of zeros lie within a distance ε of the critical line. <a href="#CITEREFIvi.C4.871985">Ivić (1985)</a> gives several more precise versions of this result, called <i>zero density estimates</i>, which bound the number of zeros in regions with imaginary part at most <i>T</i> and real part at least 1/2+ε.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hardy.E2.80.93Littlewood_conjectures">Hardy–Littlewood conjectures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Hardy–Littlewood conjectures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1914 <a href="/wiki/G._H._Hardy" title="G. H. Hardy">Godfrey Harold Hardy</a> proved that <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>Let <i>N</i>(<i>T</i>) be the total number of real zeros, <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Selberg.27s_zeta_function_conjecture">Selberg's zeta function conjecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Selberg's zeta function conjecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Selberg%27s_zeta_function_conjecture" title="Selberg's zeta function conjecture">Selberg's zeta function conjecture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Atle_Selberg" title="Atle Selberg">Atle Selberg</a> (<a href="#CITEREFSelberg1942">1942</a>) investigated the problem of Hardy–Littlewood <b>2</b> and proved that for any ε &gt; 0 there exists such <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

A. Karatsuba</a> (<a href="#CITEREFKaratsuba1984a">1984a</a>, <a href="#CITEREFKaratsuba1984b">1984b</a>, <a href="#CITEREFKaratsuba1985">1985</a>) proved that for a fixed ε satisfying the condition 0 &lt; ε &lt; 0.001, a sufficiently large <i>T</i> and <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mstyle>

The estimates of Selberg and Karatsuba can not be improved in respect of the order of growth as <i>T</i> → ∞.</p>
<p><a href="#CITEREFKaratsuba1992">Karatsuba (1992)</a> proved that an analog of the Selberg conjecture holds for almost all intervals (<i>T</i>, <i>T</i>+<i>H</i>], <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>H</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<msup>
<mi>T</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>ε<!-- ε --></mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle H=T^{\varepsilon }}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="H = T^{\varepsilon}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/785b85d331f3ca9a48ffdc4dbc3f711e609934c0" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:7.919ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span>, where ε is an arbitrarily small fixed positive number. The Karatsuba method permits to investigate zeros of the Riemann zeta-function on "supershort" intervals of the critical line, that is, on the intervals (<i>T</i>, <i>T</i>+<i>H</i>], the length <i>H</i> of which grows slower than any, even arbitrarily small degree <i>T</i>. In particular, he proved that for any given numbers ε, <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<p>The function</p>
<dl>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \pi ^{-{\frac {s}{2}}}\Gamma ({\tfrac {s}{2}})\zeta (s)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\pi^{-\frac{s}{2}}\Gamma(\tfrac{s}{2})\zeta(s)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/865be83472ebb0517bcf64a73a9a4411fa8c82e7" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:13.368ex; height:4.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>has the same zeros as the zeta function in the critical strip, and is real on the critical line because of the functional equation, so one can prove the existence of zeros exactly on the real line between two points by checking numerically that the function has opposite signs at these points. Usually one writes</p>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ζ<!-- ζ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mn>2</mn>
</mfrac>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>Z</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>i</mi>
<mi>θ<!-- θ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mrow>
</msup>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \zeta ({\tfrac {1}{2}}+it)=Z(t)e^{-i\theta (t)}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\zeta(\tfrac{1}{2} +it) = Z(t)e^{-i\theta(t)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ed8c3fcf872d4de15e1b659ca9cd5808332489bb" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:22.456ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where Hardy's function <a href="/wiki/Z_function" title="Z function"><i>Z</i></a> and the <a href="/wiki/Riemann%E2%80%93Siegel_theta_function" title="Riemann–Siegel theta function">Riemann–Siegel theta function</a> θ are uniquely defined by this and the condition that they are smooth real functions with θ(0)=0. By finding many intervals where the function <i>Z</i> changes sign one can show that there are many zeros on the critical line. To verify the Riemann hypothesis up to a given imaginary part <i>T</i> of the zeros, one also has to check that there are no further zeros off the line in this region. This can be done by calculating the total number of zeros in the region and checking that it is the same as the number of zeros found on the line. This allows one to verify the Riemann hypothesis computationally up to any desired value of <i>T</i> (provided all the zeros of the zeta function in this region are simple and on the critical line).</p>
<p>Some calculations of zeros of the zeta function are listed below. So far all zeros that have been checked are on the critical line and are simple. (A multiple zero would cause problems for the zero finding algorithms, which depend on finding sign changes between zeros.) For tables of the zeros, see <a href="#CITEREFHaselgroveMiller1960">Haselgrove &amp; Miller (1960)</a> or <a href="#CITEREFOdlyzko">Odlyzko</a>.</p>

<tr>
<th>Year</th>
<th>Number of zeros</th>
<th>Author</th>
</tr>

<td>1859?</td>
<td>3</td>
<td>B. Riemann used the <a href="/wiki/Riemann%E2%80%93Siegel_formula" title="Riemann–Siegel formula">Riemann–Siegel formula</a> (unpublished, but reported in <a href="#CITEREFSiegel1932">Siegel 1932</a>).</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>1903</td>
<td>15</td>
<td>J. P. <a href="#CITEREFGram1903">Gram (1903)</a> used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euler%E2%80%93Maclaurin_summation" title="Euler–Maclaurin summation">Euler–Maclaurin summation</a> and discovered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram%27s_law" title="Gram's law">Gram's law</a>. He showed that all 10 zeros with imaginary part at most 50 range lie on the critical line with real part 1/2 by computing the sum of the inverse 10th powers of the roots he found.</td>

<td>1925</td>
<td>138 (γ<sub><i>n</i></sub> ≤ 300)</td>
<td>J. I. <a href="#CITEREFHutchinson1925">Hutchinson (1925)</a> found the first failure of Gram's law, at the Gram point <i>g</i><sub>126</sub>.</td>

<tr>
<td>1935</td>
<td>195</td>
<td>E. It takes about O(<i>T</i><sup>3/2+ε</sup>) steps to check zeros with imaginary part less than <i>T</i>, while the Euler–Maclaurin method takes about O(<i>T</i><sup>2+ε</sup>) steps.</td>

<td>1936</td>
<td>1041</td>
<td>E. C. <a href="#CITEREFTitchmarsh1936">Titchmarsh (1936)</a> and L. J. Comrie were the last to find zeros by hand.</td>

<tr>
<td>1953</td>
<td>1104</td>
<td>A. M. <a href="#CITEREFTuring1953">Turing (1953)</a> found a more efficient way to check that all zeros up to some point are accounted for by the zeros on the line, by checking that <i>Z</i> has the correct sign at several consecutive Gram points and using the fact that <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>) has average value 0. This requires almost no extra work because the sign of <i>Z</i> at Gram points is already known from finding the zeros, and is still the usual method used. This was the first use of a digital computer to calculate the zeros.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>1956</td>

<td>D. This is called "Lehmer's phenomenon", and first occurs at the zeros with imaginary parts 7005.063 and 7005.101, which differ by only .04 while the average gap between other zeros near this point is about 1.</td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gram_points">Gram points</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Gram points">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gram_point" title="Gram point">Gram point</a> is a point on the critical line 1/2 + <i>it</i> where the zeta function is real and non-zero. Using the expression for the zeta function on the critical line, ζ(1/2 + <i>it</i>) = <i>Z</i>(<i>t</i>)e<sup> − <i>i</i>θ(<i>t</i>)</sup>, where Hardy's function, <a href="/wiki/Z_function" title="Z function"><i>Z</i></a>, is real for real <i>t</i>, and θ is the <a href="/wiki/Riemann%E2%80%93Siegel_theta_function" title="Riemann–Siegel theta function">Riemann–Siegel theta function</a>, we see that zeta is real when sin(θ(<i>t</i>)) = 0. This implies that θ(<i>t</i>) is an integer multiple of π, which allows for the location of Gram points to be calculated fairly easily by inverting the formula for θ. They are usually numbered as <i>g</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> for <i>n</i> = 0, 1, ..., where <i>g</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> is the unique solution of θ(<i>t</i>) = <i>n</i>π.</p>
<p>Gram observed that there was often exactly one zero of the zeta function between any two Gram points; Hutchinson called this observation <b>Gram's law</b>. There are several other closely related statements that are also sometimes called Gram's law: for example, (−1)<sup><i>n</i></sup><i>Z</i>(<i>g</i><sub><i>n</i></sub>) is usually positive, or <i>Z</i>(<i>t</i>) usually has opposite sign at consecutive Gram points. The imaginary parts γ<sub><i>n</i></sub> of the first few zeros (in blue) and the first few Gram points <i>g</i><sub><i>n</i></sub> are given in the following table</p>

<p>The first failure of Gram's law occurs at the 127'th zero and the Gram point <i>g</i><sub>126</sub>, which are in the "wrong" order.</p>

<td>279.148</td>
<td style="background: #eef">279.229</td>
<td>280.802</td>
<td>282.455</td>
<th style="background: #eef">282.465</th>

<td style="background: #eef">284.836</td>

<p>A Gram point <i>t</i> is called good if the zeta function is positive at 1/2 + <i>it</i>. The indices of the "bad" Gram points where <i>Z</i> has the "wrong" sign are 126, 134, 195, 211, ... (sequence <span class="nowrap"><a class="extiw" href="//oeis.org/A114856" title="oeis:A114856">A114856</a></span> in the <a href="/wiki/On-Line_Encyclopedia_of_Integer_Sequences" title="On-Line Encyclopedia of Integer Sequences">OEIS</a>). A <b>Gram block</b> is an interval bounded by two good Gram points such that all the Gram points between them are bad. A refinement of Gram's law called Rosser's rule due to <a href="#CITEREFRosserYoheSchoenfeld1969">Rosser, Yohe &amp; Schoenfeld (1969)</a> says that Gram blocks often have the expected number of zeros in them (the same as the number of Gram intervals), even though some of the individual Gram intervals in the block may not have exactly one zero in them. For example, the interval bounded by <i>g</i><sub>125</sub> and <i>g</i><sub>127</sub> is a Gram block containing a unique bad Gram point <i>g</i><sub>126</sub>, and contains the expected number 2 of zeros although neither of its two Gram intervals contains a unique zero. Rosser et al. checked that there were no exceptions to Rosser's rule in the first 3 million zeros, although there are infinitely many exceptions to Rosser's rule over the entire zeta function.</p>
<p>Gram's rule and Rosser's rule both say that in some sense zeros do not stray too far from their expected positions. The distance of a zero from its expected position is controlled by the function <i>S</i> defined above, which grows extremely slowly: its average value is of the order of (log log <i>T</i>)<sup>1/2</sup>, which only reaches 2 for T around 10<sup>24</sup>. This means that both rules hold most of the time for small <i>T</i> but eventually break down often. Indeed, <a href="#CITEREFTrudgian2011">Trudgian (2011)</a> showed that both Gram's law and Rosser's rule fail in a positive proportion of cases. To be specific, it is expected that in about 73% one zero is enclosed by two successive Gram points, but in 14% no zero and in 13% two zeros are in such a Gram-interval on the long run.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arguments_for_and_against_the_Riemann_hypothesis">Arguments for and against the Riemann hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Riemann_hypothesis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Arguments for and against the Riemann hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mathematical papers about the Riemann hypothesis tend to be cautiously noncommittal about its truth. Of authors who express an opinion, most of them, such as <a href="#CITEREFRiemann1859">Riemann (1859)</a> or <a href="#CITEREFBombieri2000">Bombieri (2000)</a>, imply that they expect (or at least hope) that it is true. The few authors who express serious doubt about it include <a href="#CITEREFIvi.C4.872008">Ivić (2008)</a> who lists some reasons for being skeptical, and <a href="#CITEREFLittlewood1962">Littlewood (1962)</a> who flatly states that he believes it is false, and that there is no evidence whatever for it and no imaginable reason that it would be true. The consensus of the survey articles (<a href="#CITEREFBombieri2000">Bombieri 2000</a>, <a href="#CITEREFConrey2003">Conrey 2003</a>, and <a href="#CITEREFSarnak2005">Sarnak 2005</a>) is that the evidence for it is strong but not overwhelming, so that while it is probably true there is some reasonable doubt about it.</p>
<p>Some of the arguments for (or against) the Riemann hypothesis are listed by <a href="#CITEREFSarnak2005">Sarnak (2005)</a>, <a href="#CITEREFConrey2003">Conrey (2003)</a>, and <a href="#CITEREFIvi.C4.872008">Ivić (2008)</a>, and include the following reasons.</p>
<ul>
<li>Several analogues of the Riemann hypothesis have already been proved. The proof of the Riemann hypothesis for varieties over finite fields by <a href="#CITEREFDeligne1974">Deligne (1974)</a> is possibly the single strongest theoretical reason in favor of the Riemann hypothesis. This provides some evidence for the more general conjecture that all zeta functions associated with automorphic forms satisfy a Riemann hypothesis, which includes the classical Riemann hypothesis as a special case. Similarly <a href="/wiki/Selberg_zeta_function" title="Selberg zeta function">Selberg zeta functions</a> satisfy the analogue of the Riemann hypothesis, and are in some ways similar to the Riemann zeta function, having a functional equation and an infinite product expansion analogous to the Euler product expansion. However, there are also some major differences; for example they are not given by Dirichlet series. The Riemann hypothesis for the <a href="/wiki/Goss_zeta_function" title="Goss zeta function">Goss zeta function</a> was proved by <a href="#CITEREFSheats1998">Sheats (1998)</a>. In contrast to these positive examples, however, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epstein_zeta_function" title="Epstein zeta function">Epstein zeta functions</a> do not satisfy the Riemann hypothesis, even though they have an infinite number of zeros on the critical line (<a href="#CITEREFTitchmarsh1986">Titchmarsh 1986</a>). These functions are quite similar to the Riemann zeta function, and have a Dirichlet series expansion and a functional equation, but the ones known to fail the Riemann hypothesis do not have an Euler product and are not directly related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automorphic_representation" title="Automorphic representation">automorphic representations</a>.</li>
<li>At first, the numerical verification that many zeros lie on the line seems strong evidence for it. However, analytic number theory has had many conjectures supported by large amounts of numerical evidence that turn out to be false. See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skewes_number" title="Skewes number">Skewes number</a> for a notorious example, where the first exception to a plausible conjecture related to the Riemann hypothesis probably occurs around 10<sup>316</sup>; a counterexample to the Riemann hypothesis with imaginary part this size would be far beyond anything that can currently be computed using a direct approach. The problem is that the behavior is often influenced by very slowly increasing functions such as log log <i>T</i>, that tend to infinity, but do so so slowly that this cannot be detected by computation. Such functions occur in the theory of the zeta function controlling the behavior of its zeros; for example the function <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>) above has average size around (log log <i>T</i>)<sup>1/2</sup> . As <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>) jumps by at least 2 at any counterexample to the Riemann hypothesis, one might expect any counterexamples to the Riemann hypothesis to start appearing only when <i>S</i>(<i>T</i>) becomes large. It is never much more than 3 as far as it has been calculated, but is known to be unbounded, suggesting that calculations may not have yet reached the region of typical behavior of the zeta function.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arnaud_Denjoy" title="Arnaud Denjoy">Denjoy</a>'s probabilistic argument for the Riemann hypothesis (<a href="#CITEREFEdwards1974">Edwards 1974</a>) is based on the observation that if μ(<i>x</i>) is a random sequence of "1"s and "−1"s then, for every <span class="nowrap">ε &gt; 0</span>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_sum" title="Partial sum">partial sums</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</dl>
</dd>
<dd>(the values of which are positions in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_random_walk" title="Simple random walk">simple random walk</a>) satisfy the bound
<dl>
<dd><span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mi>ε<!-- ε --></mi>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle M(x)=O(x^{1/2+\varepsilon })}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="M(x) = O(x^{1/2+\varepsilon})" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2c75c3dd2aa902d62979d1d0546b7d811daca627" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.466ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
<dd>with <a href="/wiki/Almost_surely" title="Almost surely">probability 1</a>. The Riemann hypothesis is equivalent to this bound for the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6bius_function" title="Möbius function">Möbius function</a> μ and the <a href="/wiki/Mertens_function" title="Mertens function">Mertens function</a> <i>M</i> derived in the same way from it. In other words, the Riemann hypothesis is in some sense equivalent to saying that μ(<i>x</i>) behaves like a random sequence of coin tosses. When μ(<i>x</i>) is non-zero its sign gives the parity of the number of prime factors of <i>x</i>, so informally the Riemann hypothesis says that the parity of the number of prime factors of an integer behaves randomly. Such probabilistic arguments in number theory often give the right answer, but tend to be very hard to make rigorous, and occasionally give the wrong answer for some results, such as <a href="/wiki/Maier%27s_theorem" title="Maier's theorem">Maier's theorem</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>The calculations in <a href="#CITEREFOdlyzko1987">Odlyzko (1987)</a> show that the zeros of the zeta function behave very much like the eigenvalues of a random Hermitian matrix, suggesting that they are the eigenvalues of some self-adjoint operator, which would imply the Riemann hypothesis. However all attempts to find such an operator have failed.</li>
<li>There are several theorems, such as <a href="/wiki/Goldbach%27s_weak_conjecture" title="Goldbach's weak conjecture">the weak Goldbach conjecture</a> for sufficiently large odd numbers, that were first proved using the generalized Riemann hypothesis, and later shown to be true unconditionally. This could be considered as weak evidence for the generalized Riemann hypothesis, as several of its "predictions" turned out to be true.</li>
<li>Lehmer's phenomenon (<a href="#CITEREFLehmer1956">Lehmer 1956</a>) where two zeros are sometimes very close is sometimes given as a reason to disbelieve in the Riemann hypothesis. However one would expect this to happen occasionally just by chance even if the Riemann hypothesis were true, and Odlyzko's calculations suggest that nearby pairs of zeros occur just as often as predicted by <a href="/wiki/Montgomery%27s_pair_correlation_conjecture" title="Montgomery's pair correlation conjecture">Montgomery's conjecture</a>.</li>
<li><a href="#CITEREFPatterson1988">Patterson (1988)</a> suggests that the most compelling reason for the Riemann hypothesis for most mathematicians is the hope that primes are distributed as regularly as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<li><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFOdlyzko"><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Odlyzko" title="Andrew Odlyzko">Odlyzko, Andrew</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.dtc.umn.edu/~odlyzko/" rel="nofollow"><i>Home page</i></a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARiemann+hypothesis&amp;rft.aufirst=Andrew&amp;rft.aulast=Odlyzko&amp;rft.btitle=Home+page&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.dtc.umn.edu%2F~odlyzko%2F&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> including <a class="external text" href="http://www.dtc.umn.edu/~odlyzko/doc/zeta.html" rel="nofollow">papers on the zeros of the zeta function</a> and <a class="external text" href="http://www.dtc.umn.edu/~odlyzko/zeta_tables/index.html" rel="nofollow">tables of the zeros of the zeta function</a></li>
<li><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFOdlyzko2002"><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Odlyzko" title="Andrew Odlyzko">Odlyzko, Andrew</a> (2002), <a class="external text" href="http://www.dtc.umn.edu/~odlyzko/talks/riemann-conjectures.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>Zeros of the Riemann zeta function: Conjectures and computations</i></a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARiemann+hypothesis&amp;rft.aufirst=Andrew&amp;rft.aulast=Odlyzko&amp;rft.btitle=Zeros+of+the+Riemann+zeta+function%3A+Conjectures+and+computations&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.dtc.umn.edu%2F~odlyzko%2Ftalks%2Friemann-conjectures.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Slides of a talk</li>
<li><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFPegg2004"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ed_Pegg,_Jr." title="Ed Pegg, Jr.">Pegg, Ed</a> (2004), <a class="external text" href="http://www.maa.org/editorial/mathgames/mathgames_10_18_04.html" rel="nofollow"><i>Ten Trillion Zeta Zeros</i></a>, Math Games website</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARiemann+hypothesis&amp;rft.aufirst=Ed&amp;rft.aulast=Pegg&amp;rft.btitle=Ten+Trillion+Zeta+Zeros&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.maa.org%2Feditorial%2Fmathgames%2Fmathgames_10_18_04.html&amp;rft.pub=Math+Games+website&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>. <li><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFPugh">Pugh, Glen, <a class="external text" href="http://web.viu.ca/pughg/RiemannZeta/RiemannZetaLong.html" rel="nofollow"><i>Java applet for plotting Z(t)</i></a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARiemann+hypothesis&amp;rft.aufirst=Glen&amp;rft.aulast=Pugh&amp;rft.btitle=Java+applet+for+plotting+Z%28t%29&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fweb.viu.ca%2Fpughg%2FRiemannZeta%2FRiemannZetaLong.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
<li><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFRubinstein">Rubinstein, Michael, <a class="external text" href="http://pmmac03.math.uwaterloo.ca/~mrubinst/l_function_public/L.html" rel="nofollow"><i>algorithm for generating the zeros</i></a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARiemann+hypothesis&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael&amp;rft.aulast=Rubinstein&amp;rft.btitle=algorithm+for+generating+the+zeros&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fpmmac03.math.uwaterloo.ca%2F~mrubinst%2Fl_function_public%2FL.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>.</li>
